<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Indigenous peoples</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ethnic groups descended from and identified with the original inhabitants of a given region</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"First peoples" redirects here. For the study of human origins, see <a href="/wiki/Anthropogeny" title="Anthropogeny">Anthropogeny</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Indigenous studies" redirects here. For the academic field studying the Indigenous peoples of North America, see <a href="/wiki/Native_American_studies" title="Native American studies">Native American studies</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Natives" redirects here. For the book by Akala, see <a href="/wiki/Natives:_Race_and_Class_in_the_Ruins_of_Empire" title="Natives: Race and Class in the Ruins of Empire">Natives: Race and Class in the Ruins of Empire</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cakchiquel_family.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2208" data-file-width="3279" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cakchiquel_family.JPG/270px-Cakchiquel_family.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cakchiquel_family.JPG/405px-Cakchiquel_family.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Cakchiquel_family.JPG/540px-Cakchiquel_family.JPG 2x" width="270"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cakchiquel_family.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya</a> family in the hamlet of Patzutzun, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, 1993</div></div></div>
<p><b>Indigenous peoples</b>, also referred to as <b>First peoples</b>, <b>First nations</b>, <b>Aboriginal peoples</b>, <b>Native peoples</b>, <b>Indigenous natives</b>, or <b>Autochthonous peoples</b> (these terms are often capitalized when referring to specific indigenous peoples as <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a>, nations, and the members of these groups),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APstyle_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-APstyle-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndigTermGuide_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IndigTermGuide-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAJA-styleguide_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAJA-styleguide-3">[3]</a></sup> are culturally distinct ethnic groups whose members are directly descended from the earliest known inhabitants of a particular geographic region and, to some extent, maintain the language and culture of those original peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The term <i>Indigenous</i> was first, in its modern context, used by Europeans, who used it to differentiate the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">European</a> settlers of the Americas and from the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">Africans</a> who were brought to the Americas as <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslaved people</a>. The term may have first been used in this context by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a> in 1646, who stated "and although in many parts thereof there be at present swarms of <i>Negroes</i> serving under the <i>Spaniard</i>, yet were they all transported from <i>Africa</i>, since the discovery of <i>Columbus</i>; and are not indigenous or proper natives of <i>America</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Peoples are usually described as "Indigenous" when they maintain traditions or other aspects of an early culture that is associated with the first inhabitants of a given region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-7">[7]</a></sup> Not all indigenous peoples share this characteristic, as many have adopted substantial elements of a colonizing culture, such as dress, religion or language. Indigenous peoples may be settled in a given region (<a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a>), exhibit a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadic" title="Nomadic">nomadic</a> lifestyle across a large territory, or be <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">resettled</a>, but they are generally historically associated with a specific territory on which they depend. Indigenous societies are found in every inhabited climate zone and continent of the world except Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> There are approximately five thousand Indigenous <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a> throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:16-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indigenous peoples' homelands have historically been colonized by larger ethnic groups, who justified colonization with beliefs of racial and religious superiority, land use or economic opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup> Thousands of Indigenous nations throughout the world are currently living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countries" title="Countries">countries</a> where they are not a majority ethnic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Indigenous peoples continue to face threats to their sovereignty, economic well-being, languages, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_knowledge" title="Indigenous knowledge">ways of knowing</a>, and access to the resources on which their cultures depend. <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_rights" title="Indigenous rights">Indigenous rights</a> have been set forth in international law by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanders,_Douglas_1999._pp._4_-_13_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanders,_Douglas_1999._pp._4_-_13-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2007, the UN issued a <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Rights_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples">Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples</a> (UNDRIP) to guide member-state national policies to the collective rights of Indigenous peoples, including their rights to protect their cultures, identities, languages, ceremonies, and access to employment, health, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_4" title="Sustainable Development Goal 4">education</a> and natural resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of the total global population of Indigenous peoples usually range from 250 million to 600 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Official designations and terminology of who is considered Indigenous vary between <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">countries</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Settler_state" title="Settler state">settler states</a> colonized by Europeans, such as in <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">the Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, Indigenous status is generally unproblematically applied to groups directly descended from the peoples who have lived there prior to European settlement. In <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and Africa, where the majority of Indigenous peoples live, Indigenous population figures are less clear and may fluctuate dramatically as states tend to underreport the population of Indigenous peoples, or define them by different terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Indigenous</i> is derived from the Latin word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">indigena</i></span>, meaning "sprung from the land, native".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Latin <i>indigena</i> is based on the <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> <i>indu</i> "in, within" + <i>gignere</i> "to beget, produce". <i>Indu</i> is an extended form of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> <i>en</i> or "in".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The origins of the term <i>indigenous</i> are not related in any way to the origins of the term <i>Indian</i>, which, until recently, was commonly applied to <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples of the Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Autochthonous</i> originates from the Greek αὐτός autós meaning self/own, and χθών chthon meaning Earth. The term is based in the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root <i>dhghem-</i> (earth). The earliest documented use of this term was in 1804.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amis_postcard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="645" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Amis_postcard.jpg/170px-Amis_postcard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Amis_postcard.jpg/255px-Amis_postcard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Amis_postcard.jpg/340px-Amis_postcard.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amis_postcard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colorized photograph of an <a href="/wiki/Amis_people" title="Amis people">Amis</a> couple in traditional clothing. Taken in pre-World War II Japanese-ruled Taiwan.</div></div></div>
<p>As a reference to a group of people, the term <i>indigenous</i> first came into use by Europeans who used it to differentiate the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">enslaved</a> <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">Africans</a>. It may have first been used in this context by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a>. In Chapter 10 of <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudodoxia_Epidemica" title="Pseudodoxia Epidemica">Pseudodoxia Epidemica</a></i> (1646) entitled "Of the Blackness of Negroes", Browne wrote "and although in many parts thereof there be at present swarms of <i>Negroes</i> serving under the <i>Spaniard</i>, yet were they all transported from <i>Africa</i>, since the discovery of <i>Columbus</i>; and are not indigenous or proper natives of <i>America</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, the term was used as a way of linking the experiences, issues, and struggles of groups of colonized people across international borders. At this time 'indigenous people(s)' also began to be used to describe a legal category in Indigenous law created in international and national legislation. The use of the 's' in 'peoples' recognizes that there are real differences between different Indigenous peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Anaya" title="James Anaya">James Anaya</a>, former Special Rapporteur on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples, defined Indigenous peoples as "living descendants of pre-invasion inhabitants of lands now dominated by others. They are culturally distinct groups that find themselves engulfed by other settler societies born of forces of empire and conquest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_definitions">National definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: National definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout history, different states designate the groups within their boundaries that are recognized as indigenous peoples according to international or national legislation by different terms. Indigenous people also include people indigenous based on their descent from populations that inhabited the country when non-Indigenous religions and cultures arrived—or at the establishment of present state boundaries—who retain some or all of their own social, economic, cultural and political institutions, but who may have been displaced from their traditional domains or who may have resettled outside their ancestral domains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cidh.org_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-cidh.org-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The status of the Indigenous groups in the subjugated relationship can be characterized in most instances as an effectively marginalized or isolated in comparison to majority groups or the nation-state as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Their ability to influence and participate in the external policies that may exercise <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> over their traditional lands and practices is very frequently limited. This situation can persist even in the case where the Indigenous population outnumbers that of the other inhabitants of the region or state; the defining notion here is one of separation from decision and regulatory processes that have some, at least titular, influence over aspects of their community and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_rights" title="Land rights">land rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of external laws, claims and cultural mores either potentially or actually act to variously constrain the practices and observances of an Indigenous society. These constraints can be observed even when the Indigenous society is regulated largely by its own tradition and custom. They may be purposefully imposed, or arise as unintended consequence of trans-cultural interaction. They may have a measurable effect, even where countered by other external influences and actions deemed beneficial or that promote Indigenous rights and interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cidh.org_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-cidh.org-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations">United Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: United Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chichicastenango-004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1580" data-file-width="2125" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Chichicastenango-004.jpg/220px-Chichicastenango-004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Chichicastenango-004.jpg/330px-Chichicastenango-004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Chichicastenango-004.jpg/440px-Chichicastenango-004.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chichicastenango-004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Guatemalan girls in their traditional clothing from the town of Santa Catarina Palopó on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Atitl%C3%A1n" title="Lake Atitlán">Lake Atitlán</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first meeting of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Working_Group_on_Indigenous_Populations" title="Working Group on Indigenous Populations">Working Group on Indigenous Populations</a> (WGIP) was on 9 August 1982 and this date is now celebrated as the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_of_the_World%27s_Indigenous_Peoples" title="International Day of the World's Indigenous Peoples">International Day of the World's Indigenous Peoples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1982 the group accepted a preliminary definition by José R. Martínez-Cobo, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_Rapporteur_(UN)" title="Special Rapporteur (UN)">Special Rapporteur</a> on Discrimination against Indigenous Populations:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Indigenous communities, peoples, and nations are those that, having a historical continuity with pre-invasion and pre-colonial societies that developed on their territories, consider themselves distinct from other sectors of the societies now prevailing in those territories, or parts of them. They form at present non-dominant sectors of society and are determined to preserve, develop, and transmit to future generations their ancestral territories, and their ethnic identity, as the basis of their continued existence as peoples, in accordance with their own cultural patterns, social institutions and legal systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>
The primary impetus in considering indigenous identity comes from considering the historical impacts of European colonialism. A 2009 United Nations report published by the Secretariat of the Permanent Forum on Indigenous Issues stated:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p>For centuries, since the time of their colonization, conquest or occupation, indigenous peoples have documented histories of resistance, interface or cooperation with states, thus demonstrating their conviction and determination to survive with their distinct sovereign identities. Indeed, Indigenous peoples were often recognized as sovereign peoples by states, as witnessed by the hundreds of treaties concluded between Indigenous peoples and the governments of the United States, Canada, New Zealand and others.  And yet as Indigenous populations dwindled, and the settler populations grew ever more dominant, states became less and less inclined to recognize the sovereignty of Indigenous peoples. Indigenous peoples themselves, at the same time, continued to adapt to changing circumstances while maintaining their distinct identity as sovereign peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-31">[31]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> defines Indigenous populations as follows: "communities that live within, or are attached to, geographically distinct traditional habitats or ancestral territories, and who identify themselves as being part of a distinct cultural group, descended from groups present in the area before modern states were created and current borders defined. They generally maintain cultural and social identities, and social, economic, cultural and political institutions, separate from the mainstream or dominant society or culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classical antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greek sources of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical</a> period acknowledge indigenous people whom they referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Pelasgians" title="Pelasgians">Pelasgians</a>". These people were seen by ancient writers either as the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> or as an earlier group of people who inhabited <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> before the Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The disposition and precise identity of this former group is elusive, and sources such as <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> give varying, partially mythological accounts. <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus" title="Dionysius of Halicarnassus">Dionysius of Halicarnassus</a> in his book, <i>Roman Antiquities</i>, gives a synoptic interpretation of the Pelasgians based on the sources available to him then, concluding that Pelasgians were Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dionysius1.17_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dionysius1.17-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a> society flourished between 330 BCE and 640 CE and commanded successive waves of conquests that gripped more than half of the known world at the time. But because already existent populations within other parts of Europe at the time of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> had more in common culturally speaking with the Greco-Roman world, the intricacies involved in expansion across the European frontier were not so contentious relative to indigenous issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_Church_and_doctrine_of_discovery">Catholic Church and doctrine of discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Catholic Church and doctrine of discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_and_the_Age_of_Discovery" title="Catholic Church and the Age of Discovery">Catholic Church and the Age of Discovery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="557" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG/220px-AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG/330px-AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG/440px-AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlonsoFernandezdeLugo2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alonso_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Lugo" title="Alonso Fernández de Lugo">Alonso Fernández de Lugo</a> presenting the captured <a href="/wiki/Guanches" title="Guanches">Guanche</a> kings of <a href="/wiki/Tenerife" title="Tenerife">Tenerife</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_and_Isabella" title="Ferdinand and Isabella">Ferdinand and Isabella</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_discovery" title="Doctrine of discovery">doctrine of discovery</a> is a legal and religious concept tied to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_church" title="Roman Catholic church">Roman Catholic Church</a> which rationalized and 'legalized' colonization and the conquering of Indigenous peoples in the eyes of Christianized Europeans. The roots of the Doctrine go back as far as the fifth century popes and leaders in the church who had ambitions of forming a global Christian commonwealth. The <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> (1096-1271) were based on this ambition of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_war" title="Holy war">holy war</a> against who the church saw as <a href="/wiki/Infidel" title="Infidel"><i>infidels</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a>'s writings from 1240 were particularly influential. He argued that Christians were justified in invading and acquiring infidel's lands because it was the church's duty to control the spiritual health of all humans on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Doctrine developed further in the 15th century after the conflict between the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Knights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_dynasty" title="Jagiellonian dynasty">Poland</a> to control '<a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a>' <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> (1414), the Knights argued that their claims were "authorized by papal proclamations dating from the time of the Crusades [which] allowed the outright confiscation of the property and sovereign rights of heathens." The council disagreed, stating that non-Christians had claims to rights of sovereignty and property under European <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>. However, the council upheld that conquests could 'legally' occur if non-Christians refused to comply with <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianization</a> and European natural law. This effectively meant that peoples who were not considered 'civilized' by European standards or otherwise refused to assimilate under Christian authority were subject to war and forced assimilation: "Christians simply refused to recognize the right of non-Christians to remain free of Christian dominion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup>
Christian Europeans had already begun invading and colonizing lands outside of Europe before the Council of Constance, demonstrating how the Doctrine was applied to non-Christian Indigenous peoples outside Europe. In the 14th and 15th centuries, the Indigenous peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Guanches" title="Guanches">Guanches</a>, became the subject of colonizers' attention. The Guanches had remained undisturbed and relatively 'forgotten' by Europeans until <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> began surveying the island for potential settlement in 1341. In 1344, a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> was issued which assigned the islands to <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a>, a kingdom in Spain. In 1402, the Spanish began efforts to invade and colonize the islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-37">[37]</a></sup> By 1436, a new papal edict was issued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Eugenius_IV" title="Pope Eugenius IV">Pope Eugenius IV</a> known as <i><a href="/wiki/Romanus_Pontifex" title="Romanus Pontifex">Romanus Pontifex</a></i> which authorized Portugal to convert the Indigenous peoples to Christianity and control the islands on behalf of the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup> The Guanches resisted European invasion until the surrender of the Guanche kings of <a href="/wiki/Tenerife" title="Tenerife">Tenerife</a> to Spain in 1496. The invaders brought destruction and diseases to the Guanche people, whose identity and culture disappeared as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armas1975278_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armas1975278-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="1056" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/320px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/480px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/640px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map with the main travels of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> (began in 15th century).</div></div></div>
<p>As Portugal expanded southward into North Africa in the 15th century, new edicts were added by subsequent popes which extended Portuguese authority over Indigenous peoples. In 1455, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">Pope Nicholas V</a> re-issued the <i>Romanus Pontifex</i> with more direct language, authorizing Portugal "to invade, search out, capture, vanquish, and subdue all <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">Saracens</a> and pagans" as well as allowing non-Christians to be placed in <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and have their property stolen. As stated by <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Seminar" title="Jesus Seminar">Robert J. Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacinta_Ruru" title="Jacinta Ruru">Jacinta Ruru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larissa_Behrendt" title="Larissa Behrendt">Larissa Behrendt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tracey_Lindberg" title="Tracey Lindberg">Tracey Lindberg</a>, the doctrine developed over time "to justify the domination of non-Christian, non-European peoples and the confiscations of their lands and rights." Because Portugal was granted 'permissions' by the papacy to expand in Africa, Spain was urged to move westward across the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, searching to convert and conquer Indigenous peoples in what they would understand as the '<a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>'. This division of the world between Spain and Portugal was formalized with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> in 1494.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_and_Isabella" title="Ferdinand and Isabella">King Ferdinand and Queen Isabella</a> hired <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>, who was dispatched in 1492, to colonize and bring new lands under the Spanish crown. Columbus 'discovered' a few islands in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> as early as 1493 and Ferdinand and Isabella immediately asked the pope to 'ratify' the discovery. In 1493, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> issued the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inter_caetera_divinae" title="Inter caetera divinae">Inter caetera divinai</a></i>, which affirmed that since the islands had been "undiscovered by others" that they were now under Spanish authority. Alexander granted Spain any lands that it discovered as long as they had not been "previously possessed by any Christian owner". The beginnings of European colonialism in the 'New World' effectively formalized the Doctrine of Discovery into '<a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>', which at that time meant law that was agreed upon by Spain, Portugal, and the Catholic Church. Indigenous peoples were not consulted or included in these arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-:6-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonialism_in_the_New_World">European colonialism in the New World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: European colonialism in the New World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="494" data-file-width="616" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg/220px-LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg/330px-LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg/440px-LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LienzodeTlaxcalaLamina5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a Spaniard entering <a href="/wiki/Chalco_(alt%C3%A9petl)" title="Chalco (altépetl)">Chalco</a> with three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tlaxcalans" title="Tlaxcalans">Tlaxcalan</a> soldiers and an Indigenous porter in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala" title="Lienzo de Tlaxcala">Lienzo de Tlaxcala</a> (pre-1585)</div></div></div>
<p>Spain issued the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Requirement_of_1513" title="Spanish Requirement of 1513">Spanish Requirement of 1513</a> (<i>Requerimiento</i>), a document that was intended to inform Indigenous peoples that "they must accept Spanish missionaries and sovereignty or they would be annihilated." The document was supposed to be read to Indigenous peoples so that they theoretically could accept or reject the proposal before any war against them could be waged: "the <i>Requerimiento</i> informed the Natives of their natural law obligations to hear the gospel and that their lands had been donated to Spain." Refusal by Indigenous peoples meant that, in the Spaniard's eyes, war could 'justifiably' be waged against them. Many <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">conquistadors</a> apparently feared that, if given the option, Indigenous peoples would actually accept Christianity, which would legally not permit invasion of their lands and the theft of their belongings. Legal scholars Robert J. Miller, Jacinta Rura, Larissa Behrendt, and Tracey Lindberg record that this commonly resulted in Spanish invaders reading the document aloud "in the night to the trees" or reading it "to the land from their ships". The scholars remark: "so much for <a href="/wiki/Legal_formalism" title="Legal formalism">legal formalism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> and natural law rights of New World Indigenous peoples."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:610_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:610-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Being Catholic countries in 1493, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> worked to 're-interpret' the Doctrine of Discovery to serve their own colonial interests. In the 16th century, England established a new interpretation of the Doctrine: "the new theory, primarily developed by English legal scholars, argued that the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">King Henry VII</a> of England, would not violate the 1493 papal bulls, which divided the world for the Spanish and Portuguese." This interpretation was also supported by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a>'s legal advisors in the 1580s and effectively set a precedent among European colonial nations that the first Christian nation to occupy land was the 'legal' owner and that this had to be respected in international law. This rationale was used in the colonization of what was to become the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonies</a>. <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James I</a> stated in the <a href="/wiki/First_Virginia_Charter" title="First Virginia Charter">First Virginia Charter</a> (1606) and the <a href="/wiki/Council_for_New_England" title="Council for New England">Charter to the Council of New England</a> (1620) that colonists could be given property rights because the lands were "not now actually possessed by any Christian Prince or People". English monarchs issued that colonists should spread Christianity "to those [who] as yet live in Darkness and miserable Ignorance of the true Knowledge and Worship of God, [and] to bring the Infidels and Savages, living in those parts, to human civility, and to a settled and quiet Government."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:610_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-:610-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Davidson_Bell01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="297" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/Charles_Davidson_Bell01.jpg/220px-Charles_Davidson_Bell01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/ab/Charles_Davidson_Bell01.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Davidson_Bell01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The arrival of <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Riebeeck" title="Jan van Riebeeck">Jan van Riebeeck</a> in <a href="/wiki/Table_Bay" title="Table Bay">Table Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> in 1652. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Davidson_Bell" title="Charles Davidson Bell">Charles Davidson Bell</a> (1813–1882)</div></div></div>
<p>This approach to colonization of Indigenous lands resulted in an acceleration of exploration and land claiming, particularly by France, England, and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Holland</a>. Land claims were made through symbolic "rituals of discovery" that were performed to illustrate the colonizing nation's legal claim to the land. Markers of possession such as crosses, flags, and plates claiming possession and other symbols became important in this contest to claim Indigenous lands. In 1642, Dutch explorers were ordered to set up posts and a plate that asserted their intention to establish a colony on the land. In the 1740s, French explorers buried lead plates at various locations to reestablish their 17th century land claims to <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio country</a>. The French plates were later discovered by Indigenous peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. Upon contact with English explorers, the English noted that the lead plates were monuments "of the renewal of [French] possession" of the land. In 1774, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_James_Cook" title="Captain James Cook">Captain James Cook</a> attempted to invalidate Spanish land claims to <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> by removing their marks of possession and then proceeding to set up English marks of possession. When the Spanish learned of this action, they quickly sent an explorer to reestablish their claim to the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:610_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-:610-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>European colonialists developed the legal concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_nullius" title="Terra nullius">terra nullius</a></i> (land that is null or void) or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacuum_domicilium" title="Vacuum domicilium">vacuum domicilium</a></i> (empty or vacant house) to validate their lands claims over Indigenous peoples' homelands. This concept formalized the idea that lands which were not being used in a manner that European legal systems approved of were open for European colonization. Historian <a href="/wiki/Henry_Reynolds_(historian)" title="Henry Reynolds (historian)">Henry Reynolds</a> captured this perspective in his statement that "Europeans regarded North America as a vacant land that could be claimed by right of discovery." These new legal concepts were developed in order to diminish reliance on papal authority to authorize or justify colonization claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:610_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-:610-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the 'rules' of colonization became established into legal doctrine agreed upon by between European colonial powers, methods of laying claims to Indigenous lands continued to expand rapidly. As encounters between European colonizers and Indigenous populations in the rest of the world accelerated, so did the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infectious diseases</a>, which sometimes caused local epidemics of extraordinary virulence. For example, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, and other diseases were unknown in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> Americas and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Settler_independence_and_continuing_colonialism">Settler independence and continuing colonialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Settler independence and continuing colonialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Settler_colonialism" title="Settler colonialism">Settler colonialism</a></div><p>
Although the establishment of colonies throughout the world by various European powers was intended to expand their nation's wealth and influence, settler populations in some localities became anxious to assert their own autonomy. For example, settler independence movements in the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonies</a> were successful by 1783, following the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. This resulted in the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a> as a separate entity from <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. The United States continued and expanded European colonial doctrine through adopting the Doctrine of Discovery as the law of the American federal government in 1823 with the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">US Supreme Court</a> case <i><a href="/wiki/Johnson_v._M%27Intosh" title="Johnson v. M'Intosh">Johnson v. M'Intosh</a></i>. Statements at the <i>Johnson</i> court case illuminated the United States' support for the principles of the discovery doctrine:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-41">[41]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>The United States ... [and] its civilized inhabitants now hold this country. They hold, and assert in themselves, the title by which it was acquired. They maintain, as all others have maintained, that discovery gave an exclusive right to extinguish the Indian title of occupancy, either by purchase or by conquest; and gave also a right to such a degree of sovereignty, as the circumstances of the people would allow them to exercise. ... [This loss of native property and sovereignty rights was justified, the Court said, by] the character and religion of its inhabitants ... the superior genius of Europe ... [and] ample compensation to the [Indians] by bestowing on them civilization and Christianity, in exchange for unlimited independence.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_and_distribution">Population and distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Population and distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="3380" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/300px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/450px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/600px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of <a href="/wiki/Uncontacted_peoples" title="Uncontacted peoples">uncontacted peoples</a>, around the start of the 21st century</div></div></div>
<p>Indigenous societies range from those who have been significantly exposed to the colonizing or expansionary activities of other societies (such as the <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya peoples</a> of Mexico and Central America) through to those who as yet remain in comparative isolation from any external influence (such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sentinelese_people" title="Sentinelese people">Sentinelese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jarawa_people_(Andaman_Islands)" title="Jarawa people (Andaman Islands)">Jarawa</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a>).
</p><p>Precise estimates for the total population of the world's Indigenous peoples are very difficult to compile, given the difficulties in identification and the variances and inadequacies of available census data. The United Nations estimates that there are over 370 million Indigenous people living in over 70 countries worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This would equate to just fewer than 6% of the total <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a>. This includes at least 5,000 distinct peoples<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-43">[43]</a></sup> in over 72 countries.
</p><p>Contemporary distinct Indigenous groups survive in populations ranging from only a few dozen to hundreds of thousands and more. Many Indigenous populations have undergone a dramatic decline and even extinction, and remain threatened in many parts of the world. Some have also been assimilated by other populations or have undergone many other changes. In other cases, Indigenous populations are undergoing a recovery or expansion in numbers.
</p><p>Certain Indigenous societies survive even though they may no longer inhabit their "traditional" lands, owing to migration, relocation, forced resettlement or having been supplanted by other cultural groups.  In many other respects, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transformation_of_culture" title="Transformation of culture">transformation of culture</a> of Indigenous groups is ongoing, and includes permanent loss of language, loss of lands, encroachment on traditional territories, and disruption in traditional ways of life due to contamination and pollution of waters and lands.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_economic_benefits_of_Indigenous_stewardship_of_land">Environmental and economic benefits of Indigenous stewardship of land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Environmental and economic benefits of Indigenous stewardship of land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
