<a href="/wiki/Category:Indigenous_rights" title="Category:Indigenous rights"><span style="color:black">Category</span></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Indigenous_rights" title="Template:Indigenous rights"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indigenous_rights" title="Template talk:Indigenous rights"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indigenous_rights&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg"><img alt="endorses Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous People, 2010" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/220px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/330px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/440px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The New Zealand delegation, including <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> members, endorses the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Rights_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples">Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples</a> in 2010.</div></div></div>
<p>Indigenous peoples confront a diverse range of concerns associated with their status and interaction with other cultural groups, as well as changes in their inhabited environment. Some challenges are specific to particular groups; however, other challenges are commonly experienced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>  These issues include cultural and linguistic preservation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_rights" title="Land rights">land rights</a>, ownership and exploitation of natural resources, political determination and autonomy, environmental degradation and incursion, poverty, health, and discrimination.
</p><p>The interactions between indigenous and non-indigenous societies throughout history and contemporarily have been complex, ranging from outright conflict and subjugation to some degree of mutual benefit and cultural transfer. A particular aspect of <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological study</a> involves investigation into the ramifications of what is termed <a href="/wiki/First_contact_(anthropology)" title="First contact (anthropology)"><i>first contact</i></a>, the study of what occurs when two cultures first encounter one another. The situation can be further confused when there is a complicated or contested history of migration and population of a given region, which can give rise to disputes about primacy and ownership of the land and resources.
</p><p>Wherever indigenous cultural identity is asserted, common societal issues and concerns arise from the indigenous status. These concerns are often not unique to indigenous groups. Despite the diversity of indigenous peoples, it may be noted that they share common problems and issues in dealing with the prevailing, or invading, society. They are generally concerned that the cultures and lands of indigenous peoples are being lost and that indigenous peoples suffer both discrimination and pressure to assimilate into their surrounding societies. This is borne out by the fact that the lands and cultures of nearly all of the peoples listed at the end of this article are under threat. Notable exceptions are the <a href="/wiki/Yakuts" title="Yakuts">Sakha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Komi_peoples" title="Komi peoples">Komi peoples</a> (two northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Russia" title="Indigenous peoples of Russia">indigenous peoples of Russia</a>), who now control their own autonomous republics within the Russian state, and the Canadian <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, who form a majority of the territory of <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a> (created in 1999). Despite the control of their territories, many Sakha people have lost their lands as a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Homestead_Act" title="Russian Homestead Act">Russian Homestead Act</a>, which allows any Russian citizen to own any land in the Far Eastern region of Russia. In Australia, a landmark case, <i>Mabo</i> v <i>Queensland</i> (No 2),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> saw the High Court of Australia reject the idea of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">terra nullius</i></span>. This rejection ended up recognizing that there was a pre-existing system of law practised by the Meriam people.
</p><p>
A 2009 United Nations publication says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-31">[31]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>Although indigenous peoples are often portrayed as a hindrance to development, their cultures and traditional knowledge are also increasingly seen as assets. It is argued that it is important for the human species as a whole to preserve as wide a range of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diversity" title="Cultural diversity">cultural diversity</a> as possible, and that the protection of indigenous cultures is vital to this enterprise.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights_violations">Human rights violations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Human rights violations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg/220px-Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg/330px-Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg/440px-Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indigenous_march_right_to_self-determination.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indigenous peoples march for their right to <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> in <a href="/wiki/Davao_City" title="Davao City">Davao City</a> (2008).</div></div></div>
<p>The Bangladesh Government has stated that there are "no indigenous peoples in Bangladesh".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafiq_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shafiq-142">[142]</a></sup> This has angered the indigenous peoples of <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts">Chittagong Hill Tracts</a>, Bangladesh, collectively known as the Jumma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mochta_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mochta-143">[143]</a></sup> Experts have protested against this move of the Bangladesh Government and have questioned the Government's definition of the term "indigenous peoples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChakmaRaja_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChakmaRaja-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SultanaKamal_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-SultanaKamal-145">[145]</a></sup> This move by the Bangladesh Government is seen by the indigenous peoples of Bangladesh as another step by the Government to further erode their already limited rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shams_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-shams-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindus and Chams have both experienced religious and ethnic persecution and restrictions on their faith under the current Vietnamese government, with the Vietnamese state confiscating Cham property and forbidding Cham from observing their religious beliefs. Hindu temples were turned into tourist sites against the wishes of the Cham Hindus. In 2010 and 2013 several incidents occurred in Thành Tín and Phươc Nhơn villages where Cham were murdered by Vietnamese. In 2012, Vietnamese police in Chau Giang village stormed into a Cham Mosque, stole the electric generator, and also raped Cham girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Cham in the Mekong Delta have also been economically marginalised, with ethnic Vietnamese settling on land previously owned by Cham people with state support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indonesian government has outright denied the existence of indigenous peoples within the countries' borders. In 2012, Indonesia stated that ‘The Government of Indonesia supports the promotion and protection of indigenous people worldwide ... Indonesia, however, does not recognize the application of the indigenous peoples concept ... in the country'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-survivalinternational.org_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-survivalinternational.org-149">[149]</a></sup> Along with the brutal treatment of the country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papuan_people" title="Papuan people">Papuan people</a> (a conservative estimate places the violent deaths at 100,000 people in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_New_Guinea" title="West New Guinea">West New Guinea</a> since Indonesian occupation in 1963, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papua_Conflict" title="Papua Conflict">Papua Conflict</a>) has led to <a href="/wiki/Survival_International" title="Survival International">Survival International</a> condemning Indonesia for treating its indigenous peoples as the worst in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-survivalinternational.org_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-survivalinternational.org-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Vietnamese viewed and dealt with the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Montagnard_(Vietnam)" title="Montagnard (Vietnam)">Montagnards</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Highlands_(Vietnam)" title="Central Highlands (Vietnam)">Central Highlands of Vietnam</a> as "savages", which caused a Montagnard uprising against the Vietnamese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmas_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmas-150">[150]</a></sup> The Vietnamese were originally centered around the Red River Delta but engaged in conquest and seized new lands such as Champa, the Mekong Delta (from Cambodia) and the Central Highlands during Nam Tien. While the Vietnamese received strong Chinese influence in their culture and civilization and were Sinicized, and the Cambodians and Laotians were Indianized, the Montagnards in the Central Highlands maintained their own indigenous culture without adopting external culture and were the true indigenous of the region. To hinder encroachment on the Central Highlands by Vietnamese nationalists, the term <i>Pays Montagnard du Sud-Indochinois</i> (PMSI) emerged for the Central Highlands along with the indigenous being addressed by the name Montagnard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salemink2003_1_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salemink2003_1-151">[151]</a></sup> The tremendous scale of Vietnamese Kinh colonists flooding into the Central Highlands has significantly altered the demographics of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salemink2003_2_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salemink2003_2-152">[152]</a></sup> The anti-ethnic minority discriminatory policies by the Vietnamese, environmental degradation, deprivation of lands from the indigenous people, and settlement of indigenous lands by an overwhelming number of Vietnamese settlers led to massive protests and demonstrations by the Central Highland's indigenous ethnic minorities against the Vietnamese in January–February 2001. This event gave a tremendous blow to the claim often published by the Vietnamese government that in Vietnam "There has been no ethnic confrontation, no religious war, no ethnic conflict. And no elimination of one culture by another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="2598" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/220px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/330px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/440px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indigenous protesters from <a href="/wiki/Vale_do_Javari" title="Vale do Javari">Vale do Javari</a>, one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_territory_(Brazil)" title="Indigenous territory (Brazil)">indigenous territories</a> in Brazil</div></div></div>
<p>In May 2016, the Fifteenth Session of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Permanent_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues" title="United Nations Permanent Forum on Indigenous Issues">United Nations Permanent Forum on Indigenous Issues</a> (UNPFII) affirmed that indigenous peoples are distinctive groups protected in international or national legislation as having a set of specific rights based on their linguistic and historical ties to a particular territory, prior to later settlement, development, and or occupation of a region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coates_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coates-154">[154]</a></sup> The session affirms that, since indigenous peoples are vulnerable to exploitation, <a href="/wiki/Social_exclusion" title="Social exclusion">marginalization</a>, oppression, forced <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> by nation states formed from colonizing populations or by different, politically dominant ethnic groups, individuals and communities maintaining ways of life indigenous to their regions are entitled to special protection.
</p><p>The indigenous people from <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a> community were reportedly subjected to eviction from their ancestral land to make way for a luxury game reserve by Otterlo Business Corporation in June 2022. The <a href="/wiki/Game_reserve" title="Game reserve">game reserve</a> was reportedly being set up for the royals of the United Arab Emirates also linked to OBC or the Otterlo Business Corporation. According to lawyers and human rights groups and activists, approximately 30 Maasai people were injured by security forces in the process of eviction and delimiting a land area of 1500 sq km. A 2019 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN" title="UN">UN</a> report has described OBC as a ‘UAE-based’ luxury-game hunting company, granted a license to hunt by the Tanzanian government in 1992 for “the UAE royal family to organize private hunting trips”, denying the Maasai people access to their own land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_issues">Health issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Health issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 1993, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> proclaimed the International Decade of the World's Indigenous People, and requested UN specialized agencies to consider with governments and indigenous people how they can contribute to the success of the Decade of Indigenous People, commencing in December 1994. As a consequence, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, at its Forty-seventh World Health Assembly, established a core advisory group of indigenous representatives with special knowledge of the health needs and resources of their communities, thus beginning a long-term commitment to the issue of the health of indigenous peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>The WHO notes that "Statistical data on the health status of indigenous peoples is scarce. This is especially notable for indigenous peoples in Africa, Asia and eastern Europe," but snapshots from various countries (where such statistics are available) show that indigenous people are in worse health than the general population, in advanced and developing countries alike: higher incidence of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> in some regions of Australia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> higher prevalence of poor sanitation and lack of safe water among Twa households in Rwanda;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> a greater prevalence of childbirths without <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_care" title="Prenatal care">prenatal care</a> among ethnic minorities in Vietnam;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> suicide rates among Inuit youth in Canada are eleven times higher than the national average;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rates are higher for Indigenous peoples everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first UN publication on the State of the World's Indigenous Peoples revealed alarming statistics about indigenous peoples' health. Health disparities between indigenous and non-indigenous populations are evident in both developed and developing countries. Native Americans in the United States are 600 times more likely to acquire tuberculosis and 62% more likely to commit suicide than the non-Indian population. Tuberculosis, obesity, and type 2 diabetes are major health concerns for the indigenous in developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup> Globally, health disparities touch upon nearly every health issue, including HIV/AIDS, cancer, malaria, cardiovascular disease, malnutrition, parasitic infections, and respiratory diseases, affecting indigenous peoples at much higher rates. Many causes of indigenous children's mortality could be prevented. Poorer health conditions amongst indigenous peoples result from longstanding societal issues, such as extreme poverty and racism, but also the intentional marginalization and dispossession of indigenous peoples by dominant, non-indigenous populations and societal structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism_and_discrimination">Racism and discrimination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Racism and discrimination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mokka_and_their_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mokka_and_their_house.jpg/220px-Mokka_and_their_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mokka_and_their_house.jpg/330px-Mokka_and_their_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mokka_and_their_house.jpg/440px-Mokka_and_their_house.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mokka_and_their_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Savages of Mokka and Their House in Formosa", pre-1945, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Japanese_rule" title="Taiwan under Japanese rule">Taiwan under Japanese rule</a></div></div></div>
<p>Indigenous peoples have frequently been subjected to various forms of racism and discrimination. Indigenous peoples have been denoted <i>primitives</i>, <i>savages</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> or <i>uncivilized</i>. These terms occurred commonly during the heyday of European colonial expansion, but still continue in use in certain societies in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 17th century, Europeans commonly labeled indigenous peoples as "uncivilized". Some philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> (1588-1679), considered indigenous people to be merely "savages". Others (especially literary figures in the 18th century) popularised the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Noble_savage" title="Noble savage">noble savages</a>". Those who were close to the Hobbesian view tended to believe themselves to have a duty to "civilize" and "modernize" the indigenous. Although anthropologists, especially from Europe, used<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2020)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> to apply these terms to all tribal cultures, the practice has fallen into disfavor as demeaning and is, according to many anthropologists, not only inaccurate, but dangerous.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Survival_International" title="Survival International">Survival International</a> runs a campaign to stamp out media portrayal of indigenous peoples as "primitive" or "savages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-survivalinternationalfaq_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-survivalinternationalfaq-165">[165]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friends_of_Peoples_Close_to_Nature" title="Friends of Peoples Close to Nature">Friends of Peoples Close to Nature</a> considers not only that indigenous culture should be respected as not being inferior, but also sees indigenous ways of life as offering frameworks in sustainability and as a part of the struggle within the "corrupted" western world, from which the threat<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2020)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> stems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fpcn_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-fpcn-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> (1914-1918), many Europeans came to doubt the morality of the means used to "civilize" peoples. At the same time, the anti-colonial movement, and advocates of indigenous peoples, argued that words such as "civilized" and "savage" were products and tools of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>, and argued that colonialism itself was savagely destructive. In the mid-20th century, European attitudes began to shift to the view that indigenous and tribal peoples should have the right to decide for themselves what should happen to their ancient cultures and ancestral lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_appropriation">Cultural appropriation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Cultural appropriation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cultures of indigenous peoples provides appeal for <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> advocates seeking to find ancient traditional truths, <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritualities</a> and practices to <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">appropriate</a> into their worldviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_injustice">Environmental injustice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Environmental injustice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helena_Gualinga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3919" data-file-width="3135" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Helena_Gualinga.jpg/170px-Helena_Gualinga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Helena_Gualinga.jpg/255px-Helena_Gualinga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Helena_Gualinga.jpg/340px-Helena_Gualinga.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helena_Gualinga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Helena_Gualinga" title="Helena Gualinga">Helena Gualinga</a>, an indigenous environmental and human rights activist<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></div></div></div><p> At an international level, indigenous peoples have received increased recognition of their environmental rights since 2002, but few countries respect these rights in reality. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Declaration_on_the_Rights_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples">UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples</a>, adopted by the General Assembly in 2007, established indigenous peoples' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_to_self-determination" title="Right to self-determination">right to self-determination</a>, implying several rights regarding natural resource management. In countries where these rights are recognized, land titling and demarcation procedures are often put on delay, or leased out by the state as concessions for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extractive_industries" title="Extractive industries">extractive industries</a> without consulting indigenous communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many in the United States federal government are in favor of exploiting oil reserves in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Arctic National Wildlife Refuge">Arctic National Wildlife Refuge</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwich%27in" title="Gwich'in">Gwich'in</a> indigenous people rely on herds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribou" title="Caribou">caribou</a>. Oil drilling could destroy thousands of years of culture for the Gwich'in. On the other hand, some of the <a href="/wiki/I%C3%B1upiat" title="Iñupiat">Inupiat Eskimo</a>, another indigenous community in the region, favor oil drilling because they could benefit economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" title="Industrial agriculture">industrial agricultural</a> technologies such as fertilizers, pesticides, and large plantation schemes have destroyed ecosystems that indigenous communities formerly depended on, forcing resettlement. Development projects such as dam construction, pipelines and resource extraction have displaced large numbers of indigenous peoples, often without providing compensation. Governments have forced indigenous peoples off of their ancestral lands in the name of <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a> and national park development. Indigenous women are especially affected by land dispossession because they must walk longer distances for water and fuel wood. These women also become economically dependent on men when they lose their livelihoods. Indigenous groups asserting their rights has most often resulted in torture, imprisonment, or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The building of <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a> can hurt indigenous peoples by hurting the ecosystems that provide them water, food. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Munduruku_people" title="Munduruku people">Munduruku people</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon rainforest</a> are opposing the building of <a href="/wiki/Tapaj%C3%B3s_hydroelectric_complex" title="Tapajós hydroelectric complex">Tapajós dam</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> with the help of Greenpeace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most indigenous populations are already subject to the deleterious effects of climate change. Climate change has not only environmental, but also human rights and socioeconomic implications for indigenous communities. The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> acknowledges climate change as an obstacle to <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a>, notably the fight against poverty, disease, and child mortality, in addition to environmental sustainability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_indigenous_knowledge">Use of indigenous knowledge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Use of indigenous knowledge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_knowledge" title="Traditional knowledge">Traditional knowledge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:273px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg/271px-College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg/407px-College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg/542px-College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg 2x" width="271"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:College_of_DuPage_Celebrates_Native_American_Culture_2020_54.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> dancer of the Save Our Ancestors Remains and Resources Indigenous Network Group (SOARRING) Foundation, a not-for-profit organization whose mission is to educate people about indigenous ways of life</div></div></div>
<p>Indigenous knowledge is considered as very important for issues linked with <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Nakata" title="Martin Nakata">Professor Martin Nakata</a> is a pioneer in the field of bringing indigenous knowledge to mainstream academics and media through digital documentation of unique contributions by aboriginal people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> supports using indigenous knowledge and giving to the indigenous peoples ownership of their land for protecting nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_reconstruction">Knowledge reconstruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigenous_peoples&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Knowledge reconstruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Western and Eastern <a href="/wiki/Penan_people" title="Penan people">Penan</a> are two major groups of indigenous populations in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. The Eastern Penan are famous for their resistance to loggers threatening their natural resources, specifically Sago palms and various fruit bearing trees. Because of the Penan's international fame, environmentalists often visited the area to document such happenings and learn more about and from the people there, including their perspective on the land's invasion. Environmentalists such as Davis and Henley, lacking dialectical connections needed to deeply understand the Penan, additionally lacked full knowledge of the situation's specific weight to the indigenous peoples.
</p><p>On a good intent, the two embarked on a mission to propagate conservation of the Penan's land resources, and mostly likely feeling a deep but inexpressible richness in the people's traditions, Davis and Henley were among the many who reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Traditional_knowledge" title="Traditional knowledge">indigenous knowledge</a> into fitting a Western narrative and agenda. For example, Davis and Henley romanticized and misconstrued the traditional Penan concept of molong, meaning: to preserve. Brosius observed this concept as the Penan marked trees for personal use and to preserve them for future harvesting of fruits or for materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-177">[177]</a></sup> Davis and Henley made inferences beyond the truth of this tradition in their accounts while also lumping all native groups of Malaysia into one homogeneous group with the same ideas and traditions. In other words, they made no distinction between the Eastern and Western Penan in their descriptions.
</p><p>Another common occurrence is to extend indigenous knowledge beyond its limits and into spiritually profound and sacredness. This tendency of journalists extends beyond Davis and Henley. It serves non-natives to add a narrative and value beyond that which already exists within the knowledge base of indigenous peoples, while also bridging many various gaps in understanding not understood otherwise. Not only do these false recounts of indigenous knowledge and traditions skew the beliefs of onlookers, but they also reconstruct the idea native peoples have of their own traditions by erasing its original value system and replacing it with a westernized version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20191225095213/http://www.cdi.gob.mx/index.php?option=com_content&amp;view=article&amp;id=272&amp;Itemid=58" rel="nofollow">"In the 2010 census "Indigenous" people were defined as persons who live in a household where an Indigenous language is spoken by one of the adult family members, and or people who self identified as Indigenous ("Criteria del hogar: De esta manera, se establece, que los hogares indígenas son aquellos en donde el jefe y/o el cónyuge y/o padre o madre del jefe y/o suegro o suegra del jefe hablan una lengua indígena y también aquellos que declararon pertenecer a un grupo indígena"</a>. <i>Cdi.gob.mx</i>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.cdi.gob.mx/index.php?option=com_content&amp;view=article&amp;id=272&amp;Itemid=58" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 25 December 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 April</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Cdi.gob.mx&amp;rft.atitle=In+the+2010+census+%22Indigenous%22+people+were+defined+as+persons+who+live+in+a+household+where+an+Indigenous+language+is+spoken+by+one+of+the+adult+family+members%2C+and+or+people+who+self+identified+as+Indigenous+%28%22Criteria+del+hogar%3A+De+esta+manera%2C+se+establece%2C+que+los+hogares+ind%C3%ADgenas+son+aquellos+en+donde+el+jefe+y%2Fo+el+c%C3%B3nyuge+y%2Fo+padre+o+madre+del+jefe+y%2Fo+suegro+o+suegra+del+jefe+hablan+una+lengua+ind%C3%ADgena+y+tambi%C3%A9n+aquellos+que+declararon+pertenecer+a+un+grupo+ind%C3%ADgena&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdi.gob.mx%2Findex.php%3Foption%3Dcom_content%26view%3Darticle%26id%3D272%26Itemid%3D58&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIndigenous+peoples"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110501220616/http://www.cdi.gob.mx/index.php?option=com_content&amp;view=category&amp;id=38&amp;Itemid=54" rel="nofollow">"Persons who speak an indigenous language but who do not live in such a household (Por lo antes mencionado, la Comisión Nacional Para el Desarrollo de los Pueblos Indígenas de México (CDI) considera población indígena (PI) a todas las personas que forman parte de un hogar indígena, donde el jefe(a) del hogar, su cónyuge y/o alguno de los ascendientes (madre o padre, madrastra o padrastro, abuelo(a), bisabuelo(a), tatarabuelo(a), suegro(a)) declaro ser hablante de lengua indígena. Además, también incluye a personas que declararon hablar alguna lengua indígena y que no forman parte de estos hogares"</a>. <i>Cdi.gob.mx</i>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.cdi.gob.mx/index.php?option=com_content&amp;view=category&amp;id=38&amp;Itemid=54" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 1 May 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Travis-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Travis_78-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Travis_78-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Travis_78-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Travis_78-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFTravis2009">Travis, Hannibal (2009). "The Cultural and Intellectual Property Interests of the Indigenous Peoples of Turkey and Iraq". <i>Texas Wesleyan Law Review</i>. <b>15</b>: 601. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2139%2Fssrn.1549804" rel="nofollow">10.2139/ssrn.1549804</a>. <q>The U.N. Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples requires states to provide an effective remedy to indigenous peoples deprived of their cultural, religious, or intellectual property (IP) without their free, prior and informed consent. The Declaration could prove to be an important safeguard for the indigenous peoples of Iraq and Turkey, the victims for centuries of massacres, assaults on their religious and cultural sites, theft and deterioration of their lands and cultural objects, and forced assimilation. These peoples, among them the Armenians, Assyrians, Greeks, and Yezidis of Turkey and Turkish-occupied Cyprus, and the Armenians, Assyrians, Yezidis, and Mandaeans of Iraq, have lost more than two-thirds of their peak populations, most of their cultural and religious sites, and thousands of priceless artifacts and specimens of visual art.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Texas+Wesleyan+Law+Review&amp;rft.atitle=The+Cultural+and+Intellectual+Property+Interests+of+the+Indigenous+Peoples+of+Turkey+and+Iraq&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.pages=601&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2139%2Fssrn.1549804&amp;rft.aulast=Travis&amp;rft.aufirst=Hannibal&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIndigenous+peoples"></span></span>
</li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 February</span> 2020</span>. <q>Many young people looked to American Indian traditions for alternative lifestyles, and this was to shape New Agers' and Neopagans' subsequent turn to and incorporation of indigenous peoples' practices into their own rituals and belief systems.
